      <p><b>Tanzania</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[note 2]</a></sup> <small>Swahili:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Swahili" title="Help:IPA/Swahili">[tanzaˈni.a]</a></span>), officially the <b>United Republic of Tanzania</b> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>: <i lang="sw">Jamhuri ya Muungano wa Tanzania</i>), is a country in East Africa within the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region. It borders <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> to the north; <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> to the northeast; <a href="/wiki/Comoro_Islands" title="Comoro Islands">Comoro Islands</a> and the Indian Ocean to the east; <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> to the south; <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> to the southwest; and <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> to the west. <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>, Africa's highest mountain, is in northeastern Tanzania. </p>
      <p>Many important <a href="/wiki/Hominid" class="mw-redirect" title="Hominid">hominid</a> fossils have been found in Tanzania, such as 6-million-year-old <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> hominid fossils. The genus <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a> ranged all over Africa 4 to 2 million years ago; and the oldest remains of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> are found near <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Lake Olduvai</a>. Following the rise of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> 1.8&nbsp;million years ago, humanity spread all over the Old World, and later in the New World and Australia under the species <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>. <i>H. sapiens</i> also overtook Africa and absorbed the older archaic species and subspecies of humanity. </p>
      <p>Later in the Stone and Bronze Age, prehistoric migrations into Tanzania included <a href="/wiki/South_Cushitic_languages" title="South Cushitic languages">Southern Cushitic</a> speakers who moved south from present-day Ethiopia;<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup> Eastern Cushitic people who moved into Tanzania from north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> about 2,000 and 4,000 years ago;<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nilotic_languages" title="Southern Nilotic languages">Southern Nilotes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Datooga_people" title="Datooga people">Datoog</a>, who originated from the present-day South Sudan–Ethiopia border region between 2,900 and 2,400 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 18</span></sup> These movements took place at about the same time as the settlement of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Mashariki Bantu</a> from West Africa in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> areas. They subsequently migrated across the rest of Tanzania between 2,300 and 1,700 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-18">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>German rule began in mainland Tanzania during the late 19th century when Germany formed <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a>. This was followed by British rule after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. The mainland was governed as <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(territory)" title="Tanganyika (territory)">Tanganyika</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a> remaining a separate colonial jurisdiction. Following their respective independence in 1961 and 1963, the two entities merged in 1964 to form the United Republic of Tanzania.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-19">[17]</a></sup> The countries had joined the British <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> in 1961 and Tanzania is still a member of the Commonwealth as one republic.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-20">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The United Nations estimated Tanzania's 2018 population at 56.31 million, which is slightly smaller than South Africa, making it the second most populous country located entirely south of the Equator.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-21">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-22">[20]</a></sup> The population is composed of about 120 <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups">ethnic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-East_Africa_Living_Encyclopedia_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-East_Africa_Living_Encyclopedia-23">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_groups" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguistic groups">linguistic</a>, and religious groups. The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> of Tanzania is a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> constitutional republic and since 1996 its official capital city has been <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a> where the president's office, the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a>, and all government ministries are located.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>, the former capital, retains most government offices and is the country's largest city, principal port, and leading commercial centre.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-19">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-official_website_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-official_website-25">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> Tanzania is a <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system"><i>de facto</i> one-party state</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Chama_Cha_Mapinduzi" title="Chama Cha Mapinduzi">Chama Cha Mapinduzi</a> party in power. </p>
      <p>Tanzania is mountainous and densely forested in the north-east, where <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a> is located. Three of <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Africa's Great Lakes</a> are partly within Tanzania. To the north and west lie <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, Africa's largest lake, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, the continent's deepest lake, known for its unique species of fish. To the south lies <a href="/wiki/Lake_Malawi" title="Lake Malawi">Lake Malawi</a>. The eastern shore is hot and humid, with the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a> just offshore. The <a href="/wiki/Menai_Bay_Conservation_Area" title="Menai Bay Conservation Area">Menai Bay Conservation Area</a> is Zanzibar's largest marine protected area. The <a href="/wiki/Kalambo_Falls" title="Kalambo Falls">Kalambo Falls</a>, located on the <a href="/wiki/Kalambo_River" title="Kalambo River">Kalambo River</a> at the Zambian border, is the second highest uninterrupted <a href="/wiki/Waterfall" title="Waterfall">waterfall</a> in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Kalambo_Falls_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalambo_Falls-27">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Christianity is the largest <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Tanzania" title="Religion in Tanzania">religion in Tanzania</a>, but there are also substantial <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">Animist</a> minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Over 100 different <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Tanzania" title="Languages of Tanzania">languages are spoken in Tanzania</a>, making it the most linguistically diverse country in East Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-sim_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sim-29">[27]</a></sup> The country does not have a <i>de jure</i> official language,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> although the national language is <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tzgov_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tzgov-31">[29]</a></sup> Swahili is used in <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary">parliamentary</a> debate, in the lower courts, and as a medium of instruction in primary school. English is used in foreign trade, in diplomacy, in higher courts, and as a medium of instruction in secondary and higher education;<sup id="cite_ref-sim_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sim-29">[27]</a></sup> although the Tanzanian government is planning to discontinue English as the primary language of instruction, it will be available as an optional course.<sup id="cite_ref-afkinsider.com_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afkinsider.com-32">[30]</a></sup> Approximately 10 per cent of Tanzanians speak Swahili as a first language, and up to 90 per cent speak it as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sim_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sim-29">[27]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The name "Tanzania" was created as a <a href="/wiki/Clipped_compound" title="Clipped compound">clipped compound</a> of the names of the two states that unified to create the country: <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(1961%E2%80%931964)" title="Tanganyika (1961–1964)">Tanganyika</a> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Zanzibar" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> It consists of the first three letters of the names of the two states ("Tan" and "Zan") and the suffix, "ia" to form Tanzania. </p>
      <p>The name "Tanganyika" is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a> words <i>tanga</i> ("sail") and <i>nyika</i> ("uninhabited plain", "wilderness"), creating the phrase "sail in the wilderness". It is sometimes understood as a reference to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The name of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> comes from "<a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">zenji</a>", the name for a local people (said to mean "black"), and the Arabic word "barr", which means coast or shore.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tanzania" title="History of Tanzania">History of Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Zanzibar" title="History of Zanzibar">History of Zanzibar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg/240px-Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg/360px-Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg/480px-Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2780" data-file-height="2625"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Olduvai_stone_chopping_tool_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 1.8-million-year-old stone chopping tool discovered at <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Olduvai Gorge</a> and on display at the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The indigenous populations of eastern Africa are thought to be the <a href="/wiki/Hadza_language" title="Hadza language">linguistically isolated</a> <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandawe_people" title="Sandawe people">Sandawe</a> hunter-gatherers of Tanzania.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 17</span></sup> </p>
      <p>The first wave of migration was by <a href="/wiki/South_Cushitic_languages" title="South Cushitic languages">Southern Cushitic</a> speakers who moved south from Ethiopia and Somalia into Tanzania. They are ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Iraqw_language" title="Iraqw language">Iraqw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gorowa_language" title="Gorowa language">Gorowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Burunge_language" title="Burunge language">Burunge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 17</span></sup> Based on linguistic evidence, there may also have been two movements into Tanzania of Eastern Cushitic people at about 4,000 and 2,000 years ago, originating from north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>pages 17–18</span></sup> </p>
      <p>Archaeological evidence supports the conclusion that <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nilotic_languages" title="Southern Nilotic languages">Southern Nilotes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Datooga_people" title="Datooga people">Datoog</a>, moved south from the present-day South Sudan / Ethiopia border region into central northern Tanzania between 2,900 and 2,400 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 18</span></sup> </p>
      <p>These movements took place at approximately the same time as the settlement of the iron-making <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Mashariki Bantu</a> from West Africa in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> areas. They brought with them the west African planting tradition and the primary staple of <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yams</a>. They subsequently migrated out of these regions across the rest of Tanzania between 2,300 and 1,700 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-18">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Nilotic_languages" title="Eastern Nilotic languages">Eastern Nilotic</a> peoples, including the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>, represent a more recent migration from present-day South Sudan within the past 500 to 1,500 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_17-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-17">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The people of Tanzania have been associated with the production of iron and steel. The <a href="/wiki/Pare_people" title="Pare people">Pare people</a> were the main producers of sought-after iron for peoples who occupied the mountain regions of north-eastern Tanzania.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Haya_people" title="Haya people">Haya people</a> on the western shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> invented a type of high-heat <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, which allowed them to forge <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">carbon steel</a> at temperatures exceeding 1,820&nbsp;°C (3,310&nbsp;°F) more than 1,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Travellers and merchants from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and India have visited the east African coast since early in the first millennium AD.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> was practised by some on the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a> as early as the eighth or ninth century A.D.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Medieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Bantu-speakers built farming and trade villages along the Tanzanian coast from the outset of the first millennium. Archaeological finds at <a href="/wiki/Fukuchani" title="Fukuchani">Fukuchani</a>, on the north-west coast of Zanzibar, indicate a settled agricultural and fishing community from the 6th century CE at the latest. The considerable amount of daub found indicates timber buildings, and shell beads, bead grinders, and iron slag have been found at the site. There is evidence for limited engagement in long-distance trade: a small amount of imported pottery has been found, less than 1% of total pottery finds, mostly from the Gulf and dated to the 5th to 8th century. The similarity to contemporary sites such as <a href="/wiki/Mkokotoni" title="Mkokotoni">Mkokotoni</a> and Dar es Salaam indicate a unified group of communities that developed into the first centre of coastal maritime culture. The coastal towns appear to have been engaged in Indian Ocean and inland African trade at this early period. Trade rapidly increased in importance and quantity beginning in the mid-8th century and by the close of the 10th century Zanzibar was one of the central Swahili trading towns.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Growth in Egyptian and Persian shipping from the Red Sea and the Persian Gulf revitalized Indian Ocean trade, particularly after the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> relocated to Fustat (Cairo). Swahili agriculturalists built increasingly dense settlements to tap into trade, these forming the earliest Swahili city-states. The Venda-Shona Kingdoms of Mapungubwe and Zimbabwe in South Africa and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, respectively, became a major producer of gold around this same period. Economic, social, and religious power was increasingly vested in <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa</a>, Tanzania's major medieval city-state. Kilwa controlled a number of smaller ports stretching down to modern-day Mozambique. <a href="/wiki/Sofala" title="Sofala">Sofala</a> became the major gold emporium and Kilwa grew rich off the trade, lying at the southern end of the Indian Ocean Monsoons. Kilwa's major rivals lay to the north, in modern-day Kenya, namely Mombasa and Malindi. Kilwa remained the major power in East Africa until the arrival of the Portuguese at the end of the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial">Colonial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Colonial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(territory)" title="Tanganyika (territory)">Tanganyika (territory)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:City_of_Kilwa,_1572.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg/220px-City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg/330px-City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg/440px-City_of_Kilwa%2C_1572.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1782" data-file-height="1443"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:City_of_Kilwa,_1572.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 1572 depiction of the city of <a href="/wiki/Kilwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilwa">Kilwa</a>, a UNESCO World Heritage Site.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Claiming the coastal strip, <a href="/wiki/Omani" class="mw-redirect" title="Omani">Omani</a> Sultan <a href="/wiki/Said_bin_Sultan" title="Said bin Sultan">Said bin Sultan</a> moved his capital to <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_City" title="Zanzibar City">Zanzibar City</a> in 1840. During this time, Zanzibar became the centre for the <a href="/wiki/East_African_slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="East African slave trade">east African slave trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> Between 65 and 90 per cent of the Arab-<a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> population of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> was enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> One of the most infamous slave traders on the East African coast was <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>, who was the grandson of an enslaved African. The <a href="/wiki/Nyamwezi_people" title="Nyamwezi people">Nyamwezi</a> slave traders operated under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Msiri" title="Msiri">Msiri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mirambo" title="Mirambo">Mirambo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Insoll" title="Timothy Insoll">Timothy Insoll</a>, "Figures record the exporting of 718,000 slaves from the Swahili coast during the 19th century, and the retention of 769,000 on the coast."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> In the 1890s, slavery was abolished.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg/220px-Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg/330px-Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg/440px-Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3824" data-file-height="2584"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Kuhnert_Schlacht_bei_Mahenge_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Maji_Maji_Rebellion" title="Maji Maji Rebellion">Maji Maji Rebellion</a> against <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German</a> colonial rule in 1905.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the late 19th century, Germany conquered the regions that are now Tanzania (minus Zanzibar) and incorporated them into <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> (GEA).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> The Supreme Council of the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">1919 Paris Peace Conference</a> awarded all of GEA to Britain on 7 May 1919, over the strenuous objections of Belgium.<sup id="cite_ref-Ends_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>240</span></sup> The British <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">colonial secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Alfred Milner</a>, and Belgium's minister <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">plenipotentiary</a> to the conference, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pierre_Orts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pierre Orts (page does not exist)">Pierre Orts</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_Orts" class="extiw" title="fr:Pierre Orts">fr</a>]</span>, then negotiated the Anglo-Belgian agreement of 30 May 1919<sup id="cite_ref-Belgium_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belgium-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>618–9</span></sup> where Britain ceded the north-western GEA provinces of <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda and Urundi</a> to Belgium.<sup id="cite_ref-Ends_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>246</span></sup> The conference's Commission on Mandates ratified this agreement on 16 July 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-Ends_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>246–7</span></sup> The Supreme Council accepted the agreement on 7 August 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-Belgium_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belgium-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>612–3</span></sup> On 12 July 1919, the Commission on Mandates agreed that the small <a href="/wiki/Kionga_Triangle" title="Kionga Triangle">Kionga Triangle</a> south of the <a href="/wiki/Rovuma_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Rovuma River">Rovuma River</a> would be given to <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese Mozambique</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ends_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>243</span></sup> with it eventually becoming part of independent Mozambique. The commission reasoned that Germany had virtually forced Portugal to cede the triangle in 1894.<sup id="cite_ref-Ends_49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ends-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>243</span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> was signed on 28 June 1919, although the treaty did not take effect until 10 January 1920. On that date, the GEA was transferred officially to Britain, Belgium, and Portugal. Also on that date, "Tanganyika" became the name of the British territory. </p>
      <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, about 100,000 people from Tanganyika joined the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied forces</a><sup id="cite_ref-Heale_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heale-51">[49]</a></sup> and were among the 375,000 Africans who fought with those forces.<sup id="cite_ref-MGT_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MGT-52">[50]</a></sup> Tanganyikans fought in units of the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_African_Rifles" title="King's African Rifles">King's African Rifles</a> during the <a href="/wiki/East_African_campaign_(World_War_II)" title="East African campaign (World War II)">East African Campaign</a> in Somalia and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a> against the Italians, in Madagascar against the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Vichy French">Vichy French</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Madagascar" title="Battle of Madagascar">Madagascar Campaign</a>, and in Burma against the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japanese</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign_1944%E2%80%9345" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma Campaign 1944–45">Burma Campaign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MGT_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MGT-52">[50]</a></sup> Tanganyika was an important source of food during this war, and its export income increased greatly compared to the pre-war years of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a><sup id="cite_ref-Heale_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heale-51">[49]</a></sup> Wartime demand, however, caused increased commodity prices and massive inflation within the colony.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> transformed an organisation into the politically oriented <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_African_National_Union" title="Tanganyika African National Union">Tanganyika African National Union</a> (TANU). TANU's main objective was to achieve national <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> for Tanganyika. A campaign to register new members was launched, and within a year, TANU had become the leading political organisation in the country. Nyerere became Minister of British-administered Tanganyika in 1960 and continued as prime minister when Tanganyika became independent in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>British rule came to an end on 9 December 1961, but for the first year of independence, Tanganyika had a <a href="/wiki/Governor_general" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor general">governor general</a> who represented the British monarch.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 6</span></sup> Tanganyika also joined the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">British Commonwealth</a> in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-20">[18]</a></sup> On 9 December 1962, Tanganyika became a democratic republic under an executive president.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 6</span></sup> </p>
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Revolution" title="Zanzibar Revolution">Zanzibar Revolution</a> overthrew the Arab dynasty in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>, accompanied with the slaughter of thousands of Arab Zanzibarians,<sup id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar-56">[54]</a></sup> which had become independent in 1963, the archipelago merged with mainland Tanganyika on 26 April 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> The new country was then named the <i>United Republic of Tanganyika and Zanzibar</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> On 29 October of the same year, the country was renamed the United Republic of Tanzania ("Tan" comes from Tanganyika and "Zan" from Zanzibar).<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-19">[17]</a></sup> The union of the two hitherto separate regions was controversial among many Zanzibaris (even those sympathetic to the revolution) but was accepted by both the <a href="/wiki/Nyerere" class="mw-redirect" title="Nyerere">Nyerere</a> government and the Revolutionary Government of Zanzibar owing to shared political values and goals. </p>
      <p>Following Tanganyika's independence and unification with Zanzibar leading to the state of Tanzania, President Nyerere emphasised a need to construct a national identity for the citizens of the new country. To achieve this, Nyerere provided what is regarded as one of the most successful cases of ethnic repression and identity transformation in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> With over 130 languages spoken within its territory, Tanzania is one of the most ethnically diverse countries in Africa. Despite this obstacle, ethnic divisions remained rare in Tanzania when compared to the rest of the continent, notably its immediate neighbour, Kenya. Furthermore, since its independence, Tanzania has displayed more political stability than most African countries, particularly due to Nyerere's ethnic repression methods.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg/220px-Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg/330px-Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg/440px-Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Arusha_Declaration_Monument.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Declaration_Monument" title="Arusha Declaration Monument">Arusha Declaration Monument</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1967, Nyerere's first presidency took a turn to the <a href="/wiki/Left_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Left politics">left</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Declaration" title="Arusha Declaration">Arusha Declaration</a>, which codified a commitment to socialism as well as <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">Pan-Africanism</a>. After the declaration, banks and many large industries were nationalised. </p>
      <p>Tanzania was also aligned with China, which from 1970 to 1975 financed and helped build the 1,860-kilometre-long (1,160&nbsp;mi) <a href="/wiki/TAZARA_Railway" title="TAZARA Railway">TAZARA Railway</a> from Dar es Salaam to Zambia.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> Nonetheless, from the late 1970s, Tanzania's economy took a turn for the worse, in the context of an international economic crisis affecting both developed and developing economies. </p>
      <p>From the mid-1980s, the regime financed itself by borrowing from the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> and underwent some reforms. Since then, Tanzania's gross domestic product per capita has grown and poverty has been reduced, according to a report by the World Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tanzania" title="Constitution of Tanzania">Constitution of Tanzania</a> was amended to allow multiple political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> In Tanzania's first multi-party elections, held in 1995, the ruling <a href="/wiki/Chama_Cha_Mapinduzi" title="Chama Cha Mapinduzi">Chama Cha Mapinduzi</a> won 186 of the 232 elected seats in the National Assembly, and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Mkapa" title="Benjamin Mkapa">Benjamin Mkapa</a> was elected as president.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The presidents of Tanzania since Independence have been <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> 1962–1985, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Hassan_Mwinyi" title="Ali Hassan Mwinyi">Ali Hassan Mwinyi</a> 1985–1995, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Mkapa" title="Benjamin Mkapa">Benjamin Mkapa</a> 1995–2005 <a href="/wiki/Jakaya_Kikwete" title="Jakaya Kikwete">Jakaya Kikwete</a> 2005–2015 <a href="/wiki/John_Magufuli" title="John Magufuli">John Magufuli</a> 2015–2021 and <a href="/wiki/Samia_Hassan_Suluhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Samia Hassan Suluhu">Samia Hassan Suluhu</a> since 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> After the long tenure of president Nyerere, the Constitution has a term limit, a president can serve a maximum of two terms. Each term is five years.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> Every president has represented the ruling party Chama cha Mapinduzi (CCM).<sup id="cite_ref-cfr.org_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cfr.org-68">[66]</a></sup> President Magufuli won a landslide victory and re-election in October 2020. According to the opposition, the <a href="/wiki/2020_Tanzanian_general_election" title="2020 Tanzanian general election">election</a> was full of fraud and irregularities.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 17 March 2021, President <a href="/wiki/John_Magufuli" title="John Magufuli">John Magufuli</a> died from heart complications whilst in office.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> Magufuli's vice president, <a href="/wiki/Samia_Suluhu_Hassan" title="Samia Suluhu Hassan">Samia Suluhu Hassan</a>, became Tanzania's first female president.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-71">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Tanzania" title="Geography of Tanzania">Geography of Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a>
      </div> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:348px;max-width:348px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg/180px-Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg/270px-Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg/360px-Elephant_and_Kilimanjaro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="851"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           An elephant passing by the snow-capped <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>.
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:162px;max-width:162px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg/160px-Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg" decoding="async" width="160" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg/240px-Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg/320px-Ngorongoro_Crater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Crater" class="mw-redirect" title="Ngorongoro Crater">Ngorongoro Crater</a>, the world's largest inactive and intact volcanic <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">caldera</a>.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>At 947,303 square kilometres (365,756&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-BFF_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BFF-6">[6]</a></sup> Tanzania is the 13th largest country in Africa and the 31st largest in the world, ranked between the larger Egypt and smaller Nigeria.<sup id="cite_ref-Size_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Size-72">[70]</a></sup> It borders Kenya and Uganda to the north; Rwanda, Burundi, and the Democratic Republic of the Congo to the west; and Zambia, Malawi, and Mozambique to the south. Tanzania is located on the eastern coast of Africa and has an Indian Ocean coastline approximately 1,424 kilometres (885&nbsp;mi) long.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> It also incorporates several offshore islands, including <a href="/wiki/Unguja" title="Unguja">Unguja</a> (Zanzibar), <a href="/wiki/Pemba_Island" title="Pemba Island">Pemba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mafia_Island" title="Mafia Island">Mafia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1245</span></sup> The country is the site of Africa's highest and lowest points: <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>, at 5,895 metres (19,341&nbsp;ft) above sea level, and the floor of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, at 1,471 metres (4,826&nbsp;ft) below sea level, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1245</span></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park,_Tanzania.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg/230px-Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg/345px-Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg/460px-Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park%2C_Tanzania.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1776" data-file-height="1080"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Wildebeest_Migration_in_Serengeti_National_Park,_Tanzania.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Wildebeest migration in the <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">Serengeti</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Tanzania is mountainous and densely forested in the northeast, where Mount Kilimanjaro is located. Three of <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Africa's Great Lakes</a> are partly within Tanzania. To the north and west lie <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, Africa's largest lake, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, the continent's deepest lake, known for its unique species of fish. To the southwest lies <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyasa" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Nyasa">Lake Nyasa</a>. Central Tanzania is a large plateau, with plains and arable land. The eastern shore is hot and humid, with the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Archipelago" title="Zanzibar Archipelago">Zanzibar Archipelago</a> just offshore. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Kalambo_Falls" title="Kalambo Falls">Kalambo Falls</a> in the southwestern region of <a href="/wiki/Rukwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Rukwa">Rukwa</a> is the second highest uninterrupted waterfall in Africa, and is located near the southeastern shore of Lake Tanganyika on the border with Zambia.<sup id="cite_ref-Kalambo_Falls_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalambo_Falls-27">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Menai_Bay_Conservation_Area" title="Menai Bay Conservation Area">Menai Bay Conservation Area</a> is Zanzibar's largest marine protected area. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg/390px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg.png" decoding="async" width="390" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg/585px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg/780px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1366" data-file-height="850"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_TZA_present.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Tanzania map of Köppen climate classification
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Tanzania" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate of Tanzania">Climate of Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">

      </table> 
      <p>Climate varies greatly within Tanzania. In the highlands, temperatures range between 10 and 20&nbsp;°C (50 and 68&nbsp;°F) during cold and hot seasons respectively. The rest of the country has temperatures rarely falling lower than 20&nbsp;°C (68&nbsp;°F). The hottest period extends between November and February (25–31&nbsp;°C or 77.0–87.8&nbsp;°F) while the coldest period occurs between May and August (15–20&nbsp;°C or 59–68&nbsp;°F). Annual temperature is 20&nbsp;°C (68.0&nbsp;°F). The climate is cool in high mountainous regions. </p>
      <p>Tanzania has two major rainfall periods: one is <a href="/wiki/Unimodality" title="Unimodality">uni-modal</a> (October–April) and the other is bi-modal (October–December and March–May).<sup id="cite_ref-rain_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rain-75">[73]</a></sup> The former is experienced in southern, central, and western parts of the country, and the latter is found in the north from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> extending east to the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-rain_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rain-75">[73]</a></sup> The bi-modal rainfall is caused by the seasonal migration of the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rain_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rain-75">[73]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Tanzania" title="Climate change in Tanzania">Climate change in Tanzania</a> is resulting in rising temperatures with a higher likelihood of intense rainfall events (resulting in flooding) and of dry spells (resulting in droughts).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> is already impacting the sectors in Tanzania of agriculture, water resources, health and energy. Sea level rise and changes in the quality of water are expected to impact fisheries and aquaculture.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tanzania produced a <a href="/wiki/National_Adaptation_Programme_of_Action" title="National Adaptation Programme of Action">National Adaptation Programmes of Action</a> (NAPAs) in 2007 as mandated by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a>. In 2012, Tanzania produced a National Climate Change Strategy in response to the growing concern of the negative impacts of climate change and climate variability on the country's social, economic and physical environment.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife_and_conservation">Wildlife and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wildlife and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Tanzania" title="Wildlife of Tanzania">Wildlife of Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_protected_areas_of_Tanzania" title="List of protected areas of Tanzania">List of protected areas of Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Tanzania contains around 20% of the species of Africa's enormous warm-blooded animal populace, found over its 21 National parks, reserves, 1 conservation area, and 3 marine parks. Spread over a zone of in excess of 42,000 square kilometres (16,000 sq. mi) and shaping around 38% of the nation's area.<sup id="cite_ref-Tanzania_travel_guide_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tanzania_travel_guide-80">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> Tanzania has 21 national parks,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> plus a variety of game and forest reserves, including the <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Conservation_Area" title="Ngorongoro Conservation Area">Ngorongoro Conservation Area</a>. In western Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/Gombe_Stream_National_Park" title="Gombe Stream National Park">Gombe Stream National Park</a> is the site of <a href="/wiki/Jane_Goodall" title="Jane Goodall">Jane Goodall</a>'s ongoing study of <a href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" class="mw-redirect" title="Common chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a> behaviour, which started in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tanzania is highly <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiverse</a> and contains a wide variety of animal habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> On Tanzania's <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">Serengeti</a> plain, white-bearded <a href="/wiki/Wildebeest" title="Wildebeest">wildebeest</a> (<i>Connochaetes taurinus mearnsi</i>), other <a href="/wiki/Bovid" class="mw-redirect" title="Bovid">bovids</a> and zebra<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> participate in a large-scale annual migration. Tanzania is home to about 130 <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibian</a> and over 275 reptile species, many of them strictly <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> and included in the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>'s Red Lists of countries.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> Tanzania has the largest lion population in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tanzania had a 2019 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 7.13/10, ranking it 54th globally out of 172 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-89">[87]</a></sup> </p>
