         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg/200px-Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg/300px-Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg/400px-Songo_Songo_Gas_Plant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Songo_Songo_Island" title="Songo Songo Island">Songo Songo</a> Gas Plant
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Industry and construction is a major and growing component of the Tanzanian economy, contributing 22.2 per cent of GDP in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 37</span></sup> This component includes mining and quarrying, manufacturing, electricity and natural gas, water supply, and construction.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 37</span></sup> Mining contributed 3.3 per cent of GDP in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 33</span></sup> The vast majority of the country's mineral export revenue comes from gold, accounting for 89 per cent of the value of those exports in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 71</span></sup> It also exports sizeable quantities of gemstones, including <a href="/wiki/Diamonds" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamonds">diamonds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzanite" title="Tanzanite">tanzanite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1251</span></sup> All of Tanzania's coal production, which totalled 106,000 short tons in 2012, is used domestically.<sup id="cite_ref-EIA_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-153">[151]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Only 15 per cent of Tanzanians had access to electric power in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-data.worldbank.org_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-data.worldbank.org-154">[152]</a></sup> The government-owned <a href="/wiki/TANESCO" title="TANESCO">Tanzania Electric Supply Company Limited</a> (TANESCO) dominates the electric supply industry in Tanzania.<sup id="cite_ref-EWURA-El_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-El-155">[153]</a></sup> The country generated 6.013&nbsp;billion <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt hours">kilowatt hours</a> (kWh) of electricity in 2013, a 4.2 per cent increase over the 5.771&nbsp;billion kWh generated in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-BudEx_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BudEx-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 4</span></sup> Generation increased by 63 per cent between 2005 and 2012;<sup id="cite_ref-2012Elec_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Elec-157">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup> Almost 18 per cent of the electricity generated in 2012 was lost because of theft and transmission and distribution problems.<sup id="cite_ref-2012Elec_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Elec-157">[155]</a></sup> The electrical supply varies, particularly when droughts disrupt hydropower electric generation; rolling blackouts are implemented as necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1251</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EWURA-El_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-El-155">[153]</a></sup> The unreliability of the electrical supply has hindered the development of Tanzanian industry.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1251</span></sup> In 2013, 49.7 per cent of Tanzania's electricity generation came from natural gas, 28.9 per cent from hydroelectric sources, 20.4 per cent from thermal sources, and 1.0 per cent from outside the country.<sup id="cite_ref-BudEx_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BudEx-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 5</span></sup> The government has built a 532 kilometres (331&nbsp;mi) gas pipeline from <a href="/wiki/Mnazi_Bay-Ruvuma_Estuary_Marine_Park" title="Mnazi Bay-Ruvuma Estuary Marine Park">Mnazi Bay</a> to Dar es Salaam.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> This pipeline was expected to allow the country to double its electricity generation capacity to 3,000 megawatts by 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup> The government's goal is to increase capacity to at least 10,000 megawatts by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg/220px-Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg/330px-Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg/440px-Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nyerere_Bridge_-_Kigamboni_.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Nyerere Bridge in Kigamboni, Dar es Salaam, is Tanzania's only suspension bridge.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>According to <a href="/wiki/PFC_Energy" title="PFC Energy">PFC Energy</a>, 25 to 30&nbsp;trillion cubic feet of recoverable natural gas resources have been discovered in Tanzania since 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-EIA_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-153">[151]</a></sup> bringing the total reserves to over 43&nbsp;trillion cubic feet by the end of 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup> The value of natural gas actually produced in 2013 was US$52.2&nbsp;million, a 42.7 per cent increase over 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 73</span></sup> </p>
      <p>Commercial production of gas from the Songo Songo Island field in the Indian Ocean commenced in 2004, thirty years after it was discovered there.<sup id="cite_ref-EABrief_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EABrief-163">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EWURA-Ng_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-Ng-164">[162]</a></sup> Over 35&nbsp;billion cubic feet of gas was produced from this field in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 72</span></sup> with proven, probable, and possible reserves totalling 1.1&nbsp;trillion cubic feet.<sup id="cite_ref-EWURA-Ng_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-Ng-164">[162]</a></sup> The gas is transported by pipeline to Dar es Salaam.<sup id="cite_ref-EABrief_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EABrief-163">[161]</a></sup> As of 27 August 2014, TANESCO owed the operator of this field, Orca Exploration Group Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A newer natural gas field in Mnazi Bay in 2013 produced about one-seventh of the amount produced near Songo Songo Island<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 73</span></sup> but has proven, probable, and possible reserves of 2.2&nbsp;trillion cubic feet.<sup id="cite_ref-EWURA-Ng_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWURA-Ng-164">[162]</a></sup> Virtually all of that gas is being used for electricity generation in <a href="/wiki/Mtwara" title="Mtwara">Mtwara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EABrief_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EABrief-163">[161]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Ruvuma and Nyuna regions of Tanzania have been explored mostly by the discovery company that holds a 75 per cent interest, Aminex, and has shown to hold in excess of 3.5&nbsp;trillion cubic feet of natural gas. A pipeline connecting offshore natural gas fields to Tanzania's commercial capital Dar es Salaam was completed at the end of April 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Tanzania" title="Tourism in Tanzania">Tourism in Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG/220px-Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG/330px-Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG/440px-Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Uhuru_Peak_Mt._Kilimanjaro_1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The snowcapped <a href="/wiki/Uhuru_Peak" class="mw-redirect" title="Uhuru Peak">Uhuru Peak</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Travel and tourism contributed 17.5 per cent of Tanzania's gross domestic product in 2016<sup id="cite_ref-:1_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-167">[165]</a></sup> and employed 11.0 per cent of the country's labour force (1,189,300 jobs) in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Knoema_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knoema-168">[166]</a></sup> Overall receipts rose from US$1.74&nbsp;billion in 2004 to US$4.48&nbsp;billion in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-Knoema_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knoema-168">[166]</a></sup> and receipts from international tourists rose from US$1.255&nbsp;billion in 2010 to US$2&nbsp;billion in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-167">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup> In 2016, 1,284,279 tourists arrived at Tanzania's borders compared to 590,000 in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-UND_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UND-140">[138]</a></sup> The vast majority of tourists visit <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> or a "northern circuit" of <a href="/wiki/Serengeti_National_Park" title="Serengeti National Park">Serengeti National Park</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Conservation_Area" title="Ngorongoro Conservation Area">Ngorongoro Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarangire_National_Park" title="Tarangire National Park">Tarangire National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manyara_National_Park" title="Lake Manyara National Park">Lake Manyara National Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kilimanjaro" title="Mount Kilimanjaro">Mount Kilimanjaro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1252</span></sup> In 2013, the most visited national park was Serengeti (452,485 tourists), followed by Manyara (187,773) and Tarangire (165,949).<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_55-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page xx</span></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banking">Banking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Banking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Tanzania" title="Bank of Tanzania">Bank of Tanzania</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central bank</a> of Tanzania and is primarily responsible for maintaining price stability, with a subsidiary responsibility for issuing <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_shilling" title="Tanzanian shilling">Tanzanian shilling</a> notes and coins.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup> At the end of 2013, the total assets of the Tanzanian banking industry were 19.5&nbsp;trillion Tanzanian shillings, a 15 per cent increase over 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Tanzania" title="Transport in Tanzania">Transport in Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg/180px-Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg/270px-Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg/360px-Trunk_road_Tanzania.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           One of the main trunk roads
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg/220px-Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg/330px-Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg/440px-Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1198" data-file-height="690"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zanzibar_Harbour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Zanzibar harbour
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Most transport in Tanzania is by road, with road transport constituting over 75 per cent of the country's freight traffic and 80 per cent of its passenger traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1252</span></sup> The 86,500 kilometres (53,700&nbsp;mi) road system is in generally poor condition.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1252</span></sup> Tanzania has two railway companies: <a href="/wiki/TAZARA" class="mw-redirect" title="TAZARA">TAZARA</a>, which provides service between Dar es Salaam and <a href="/wiki/Kapiri_Mposhi" title="Kapiri Mposhi">Kapiri Mposhi</a> (in a copper-mining district in Zambia), and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania_Railways_Limited" title="Tanzania Railways Limited">Tanzania Railways Limited</a>, which connects Dar es Salaam with central and northern Tanzania.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1252</span></sup> Rail travel in Tanzania often entails slow journeys with frequent cancellations or delays, and the railways have a deficient safety record.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1252</span></sup> </p>
      <p>In Dar es Salaam, there is a huge project of rapid buses, <a href="/wiki/Dar_Rapid_Transit_Agency" title="Dar Rapid Transit Agency">Dar Rapid Transit (DART)</a> which connects suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a> city. The development of the DART system consists of six phases and is funded by the African Development Bank, the World Bank and the Government of Tanzania. The first phase began in April 2012, and it was completed in December 2015 and launched operations in May 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tanzania has four international airports, along with over 120 small airports or landing strips. Airport infrastructure tends to be in poor condition.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1253</span></sup> Airlines in Tanzania include <a href="/wiki/Air_Tanzania" title="Air Tanzania">Air Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Precision_Air" title="Precision Air">Precision Air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fastjet" title="Fastjet">Fastjet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Aviation" title="Coastal Aviation">Coastal Aviation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ZanAir" title="ZanAir">ZanAir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1253</span></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Tanzania" title="Telecommunications in Tanzania">Telecommunications in Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2013, the communications sector was the fastest growing in Tanzania, expanding 22.8 per cent; however, the sector accounted for only 2.4 per cent of gross domestic product that year.<sup id="cite_ref-BudEx_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BudEx-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 2</span></sup> </p>
      <p>As of 2011, Tanzania had 56 mobile telephone subscribers per 100 inhabitants, a rate slightly above the sub-Saharan average.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1253</span></sup> Very few Tanzanians have fixed-line telephones.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1253</span></sup> Approximately 12 per cent of Tanzanians used the internet as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, though this number is growing rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1253</span></sup> The country has a fibre-optic cable network that replaced unreliable satellite service, but internet bandwidth remains very low.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1253</span></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">Water supply and sanitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Water supply and sanitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Tanzania" title="Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania">Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa,_2012_or_closest_year.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg/220px-Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg/330px-Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg/440px-Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa%2C_2012_or_closest_year.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="232" data-file-height="336"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gross_domestic_expenditure_on_Research_and_Development_GDP_ratio_in_Southern_Africa,_2012_or_closest_year.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Domestic expenditure on research in Southern Africa as a percentage of GDP, 2012 or closest year. Source: UNESCO Science Report: towards 2030 (2015), Figure 20.3
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Water supply and sanitation in Tanzania has been characterised by decreasing access to <a href="/wiki/Improved_water_source" title="Improved water source">improved water sources</a> in the 2000s (especially in urban areas), steady access to some form of sanitation (around 93 per cent since the 1990s), intermittent water supplies, and generally low quality of service.<sup id="cite_ref-mowi_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mowi-173">[171]</a></sup> Many utilities are barely able to cover their operation and maintenance costs through revenues because of low tariffs and poor efficiency. There are significant <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Tanzania" title="Regions of Tanzania">regional</a> differences, with the best performing utilities being <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moshi,_Tanzania" title="Moshi, Tanzania">Moshi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tanga,_Tanzania" title="Tanga, Tanzania">Tanga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pubexp_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pubexp-174">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The government of Tanzania has embarked on a major sector reform process since 2002. An ambitious National Water Sector Development Strategy that promotes <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Water_Resources_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Water Resources Management">Integrated Water Resources Management</a> and the development of urban and rural water supply was adopted in 2006. Decentralisation has meant that responsibility for water and sanitation service provision has shifted to local government authorities and is carried out by 20 urban utilities and about 100 district utilities, as well as by Community Owned Water Supply Organisations in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-mowi_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mowi-173">[171]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>These reforms have been backed by a significant increase of the budget starting in 2006, when the water sector was included among the priority sectors of the National Strategy for Growth and Reduction of Poverty. The Tanzanian water sector remains heavily dependent on external donors, with 88 per cent of the available funds being provided by external donor organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-NWSDS_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NWSDS-175">[173]</a></sup> Results have been mixed. For example, a report by <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Gesellschaft_f%C3%BCr_Internationale_Zusammenarbeit" title="Deutsche Gesellschaft für Internationale Zusammenarbeit">Deutsche Gesellschaft für Internationale Zusammenarbeit</a> noted that "despite heavy investments brought in by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, (the utility serving <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>) has remained one of the worst performing water entities in Tanzania."<sup id="cite_ref-GIZ_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GIZ-176">[174]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_statistics_controversy">Economic statistics controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Economic statistics controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Two articles in the <i>Economist</i> in July 2020 raised doubts about official claims of economic growth: "If Tanzania’s economy grew by almost 7% in the fiscal year to the end of June 2019, why did tax revenue fall by 1%? And why has bank lending to companies slumped? Private data are bad, too. In 2019 sales at the biggest brewer fell by 5%. Sales of cement by the two biggest producers were almost flat. None of these things is likely if growth is storming ahead. The discrepancies are so large that it is hard to avoid the conclusion that the government is lying."<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tim Staermose, a proponent of African investment, took issue with these data: "Some of these statements by <i>The Economist</i>, based on the evidence I have gathered from primary sources – namely, the statutory financial reports that listed companies in Tanzania are legally obligated to release – are simply not true. Bank lending to companies as far as I can see has not, 'slumped.' The two biggest banks in Tanzania, which between them account for approximately 40% of the banking sector, both reported strong loan growth in 2019. ... As for cement sales being 'almost flat,' again, this is total nonsense. ... In 2019 Twiga sold 6% more cement by volume than it did in 2018. In the first six months of 2020, Twiga already sold 8% more cement than it had done by the same stage in 2019. Again, these numbers are very consistent with an economy that’s reported to be growing at around 7% per annum. ... [On] the 5% fall in beer sales in 2019 ... the published 2019 annual report by Tanzania Breweries Limited (TBL) will tell you there were one-off circumstances that largely drove the decline ... [which] resulted in sales falling. But TBL's profits actually rose in 2019."<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_nutrition">Food and nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Food and nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:255px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pilua_for_many.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Pilua_for_many.jpg/253px-Pilua_for_many.jpg" decoding="async" width="253" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Pilua_for_many.jpg/380px-Pilua_for_many.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Pilua_for_many.jpg/506px-Pilua_for_many.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3610" data-file-height="2400"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pilua_for_many.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Tanzanian woman cooks Pilau rice dish wearing traditional <a href="/wiki/Kanga_(African_garment)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanga (African garment)">Kanga</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Poor nutrition remains a persistent problem within Tanzania and varies hugely throughout the country's regions. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">USAID</a> reports that 16% of children are underweight and 34% experience stunted growth as a result of malnutrition.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-180">[178]</a></sup> 10 regions house 58% of children suffering from stunted growth while 50% of acutely malnourished children can be found in 5 regions.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-181">[179]</a></sup> Over a 5-year period, the <a href="/wiki/Mara_Region" title="Mara Region">Mara</a> district of Tanzania saw a 15% reduction in stunting in children under 5 years old, falling from 46% to 31% in 2005 and 2010 respectively. <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a>, on the other hand saw a 7% increase in the prevalence of stunting in this age group, rising from 50% in 2005 to 57% in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-182">[180]</a></sup> Overall availability of food does not necessarily contribute to overall stunting figures. <a href="/wiki/Iringa_Region" title="Iringa Region">Iringa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbeya_Region" title="Mbeya Region">Mbeya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rukwa_Region" title="Rukwa Region">Rukwa</a> regions, where overall availability of food is considered acceptable still experience stunting incidences in excess of 50%. In some areas where food shortages are common such as in the <a href="/wiki/Tabora_Region" title="Tabora Region">Tabora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singida_Region" title="Singida Region">Singida</a> regions, stunting incidences remain comparatively less than those seen in Iringa, Mbeya and Rukwa.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-182">[180]</a></sup> The Tanzania Food and Nutrition Centre attributes these discrepancies to variance in maternal malnutrition, poor infant feeding practices, hygiene practices and poor healthcare services.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-182">[180]</a></sup> Periods of drought can have significant impacts on the production of crops in Tanzania. Drought in East Africa has resulted in massive increases in the prices of food staples such as maize and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_sorghum" title="Commercial sorghum">sorghum</a>, crops crucial to the nutrition of the majority of Tanzania's population. From 2015 to 2017 the price of maize when bought wholesale more than tripled, from 400 <a href="/wiki/Tanzanian_shilling" title="Tanzanian shilling">Shillings</a> per kilogram to 1253 Shillings per kilogram.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:256px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg/254px-Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg" decoding="async" width="254" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg/381px-Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg/508px-Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1080" data-file-height="720"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Farmers_harvesting_rice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Farmers using a Rice harvester to harvest rice in Igunga District, Tanzania
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Tanzania remains heavily agricultural, with 80% of the total population engaging in subsistence farming.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-184">[182]</a></sup> Rural areas are subjected to increased food shortages in comparison to urbanised areas, with a survey carried out within the country in 2017 finding 84% of people in rural areas suffering food shortages over a 3-month period compared to 64% of residents in cities.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-184">[182]</a></sup> This disparity between rural and city nutrition can be attributed to various factors; increased nutritional needs due to manual labour, more limited access to food as a result of poor infrastructure, high-susceptibility to the damaging effects of nature and the "Agricultural Productivity Gap".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> The Agricultural Productivity Gap postulates that "value added per worker" is often much lower within the agricultural sector than that found within non-agricultural sectors. Furthermore, allocation of labour within the agricultural sector is largely allocated ineffectively.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Programmes_targeting_hunger">Programmes targeting hunger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Programmes targeting hunger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>USAID programmes focussing on nutrition operate within the <a href="/wiki/Morogoro" title="Morogoro">Morogoro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iringa" title="Iringa">Iringa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbeya" title="Mbeya">Mbeya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manyara_Region" title="Manyara Region">Manyara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songwe_Region" title="Songwe Region">Songwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> regions of Tanzania. These "<a href="/wiki/Feed_the_Future_Initiative" title="Feed the Future Initiative">Feed the Future</a>" programmes heavily invest in nutrition, infrastructure, policy, capacity of institutions and agriculture which is identified by the organisation as a key area of economic growth in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-180">[178]</a></sup> A Tanzanian government led initiative "Kilimo Kwanza" or "Agriculture First" aims to encourage investment into agriculture within the private sector and hopes to improve agricultural processes and development within the country by seeking the knowledge of young people and the innovation that they can potentially provide.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-187">[185]</a></sup> During the 1990s, around 25% of Tanzania's population were provided access to iodized oil aimed to target iodine deficiency within expecting mothers, as result of studies showing the negative effects of in-utero iodine deficiency on cognitive development in children. Research showed that children of mothers with access to the supplement achieved on average greater than a third of a year more education than those who did not.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-187">[185]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:255px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg/253px-Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg" decoding="async" width="253" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg/380px-Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg/506px-Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="437"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Food_Items_in_World_Food_Programme_Food_Parcels.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Example of a World Food Programme parcel
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Programmes led by the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> operate within Tanzania. The Supplementary Feeding Programme (SFP) aims to target acute malnutrition by supplying blended food fortified with vitamins to pregnant women and mothers to children under 5 on a monthly basis.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-188">[186]</a></sup> Pregnant women and mothers to children under 2 have access to the Mother and Child Health and Nutrition Programme's "Super Cereal" which is supplied with the intent of reducing stunting in children.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-188">[186]</a></sup> World Food Programme supplementation remains the main food source for Tanzania's refugees. Super Cereal, Vegetable Oil, Pulses and Salt are supplied as part of the Protracted Relief and Recovery Operation to meet the average persons minimum daily caloric requirement of 2,100 kcal.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_188-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-188">[186]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> state that continued investment in nutrition within Tanzania is of the utmost importance: Estimates predict that Tanzania stands to lose $20 billion by 2025 if nutrition within the country remains at its current level, however improvements in nutrition could produce a gain of around $4.7 billion<sup id="cite_ref-:3_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-181">[179]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Save the Children, with the help of UNICEF and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Aid" title="Irish Aid">Irish Aid</a> funding created the Partnership for Nutrition in Tanzania (PANITA), in 2011. PANITA aims to use civil society organisations to target nutrition within the country. Alongside this, various sectors associated with nutrition are targeted such as agriculture, water, sanitation, education, economic development and social progress. PANITA is responsible for ensuring significant attention is given to nutrition in development plans and budgets created on national and regional levels within Tanzania. Since its conception, PANITA has grown from 94 to 306 participating civil society organisations nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-189">[187]</a></sup> Agriculture within Tanzania is targeted by the Irish Aid led initiative Harnessing Agriculture for Nutrition Outcomes (HANO), which aims to merge nutrition initiatives with agriculture in the Lindi District of the country. The project aims to reduce stunting by 10% in children aged 0 to 23 months.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-189">[187]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Researchers_(HC)_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants,_2013_or_closest_year.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg/220px-Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg/330px-Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg/440px-Researchers_%28HC%29_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants%2C_2013_or_closest_year.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="232" data-file-height="336"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Researchers_(HC)_in_Southern_Africa_per_million_inhabitants,_2013_or_closest_year.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Researchers (HC) in Southern Africa per million inhabitants, 2013 or closest year
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Tanzania" title="Science and technology in Tanzania">Science and technology in Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Tanzania's first "National Science and Technology Policy" was adopted in 1996. The objective of the government's "Vision 2025" (1998) document was to "transform the economy into a strong, resilient and competitive one, buttressed by science and technology". </p>
      <p>Under the umbrella of the One UN Initiative, UNESCO and Tanzanian government departments and agencies formulated a series of proposals in 2008 for revising the "National Science and Technology Policy". The total reform budget of US$10&nbsp;million was financed from the One UN fund and other sources. UNESCO provided support for mainstreaming science, technology, and innovation into the new "National Growth and Poverty Reduction Strategy" for the mainland and Zanzibar namely, Mkukuta II and Mkuza II, including in the field of tourism. </p>
      <p>Tanzania's revised science policy was published in 2010. Entitled "National Research and Development Policy", it recognises the need to improve the process of prioritisation of research capacities, develop international co-operation in strategic areas of research and development, and improve planning for human resources. It also makes provisions for the establishment of a National Research Fund. This policy was, in turn, reviewed in 2012 and 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-190">[188]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg/220px-Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="76" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg/330px-Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg/440px-Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="511" data-file-height="176"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Seychelles_and_South_Africa_have_the_most_publications_per_million_inhabitants.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Scientific&nbsp;publications per million inhabitants in SADC countries in 2014. Source: UNESCO Science Report (2015), data from Thomson Reuters' Web of Science, Science Citation Index Expanded
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2010, Tanzania devoted 0.38 per cent of GDP to research and development. The global average in 2013 was 1.7 per cent of GDP. Tanzania had 69 researchers (in head counts) per million population in 2010. In 2014, Tanzania counted 15 publications per million inhabitants in internationally catalogued journals, according to Thomson Reuters' Web of Science (Science Citation Index Expanded). The average for sub-Saharan Africa was 20 publications per million inhabitants and the global average 176 publications per million inhabitants. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Tanzania" title="Demographics of Tanzania">Demographics of Tanzania</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 10px"> 
       <caption>
        Population in Tanzania<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-21">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-22">[20]</a></sup> 
       </caption> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th scope="col" style="background:#cfb;">Year </th> 
         <th scope="col" style="background:#cfb;">Million </th>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>1950</td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">7.9 </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>2000</td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">35.1 </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>2018</td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">56.3 </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>According to the 2012 census, the total population was 44,928,923.<sup id="cite_ref-2012_census_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_census-8">[8]</a></sup> The under-15 age group represented 44.1 per cent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-nbs1_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbs1-191">[189]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The population distribution in Tanzania is uneven. Most people live on the northern border or the eastern coast, with much of the remainder of the country being sparsely populated.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_74-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 1252</span></sup> Density varies from 12 per square kilometre (31/sq&nbsp;mi) in the <a href="/wiki/Katavi_Region" title="Katavi Region">Katavi Region</a> to 3,133 per square kilometre (8,110/sq&nbsp;mi) in the <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Dar es Salaam Region">Dar es Salaam Region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2012_census_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_census-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 6</span></sup> </p>
      <p>Approximately 70 per cent of the population is rural, although this percentage has been declining since at least 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup> Dar es Salaam (population 4,364,541)<sup id="cite_ref-census2012_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census2012-193">[191]</a></sup> is the largest city and commercial capital. <a href="/wiki/Dodoma" title="Dodoma">Dodoma</a> (population 410,956)<sup id="cite_ref-census2012_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census2012-193">[191]</a></sup> is located in the centre of Tanzania, is the capital of the country, and hosts the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Tanzania)" title="National Assembly (Tanzania)">National Assembly</a>. </p>
      <p>At the time of the foundation of the United Republic of Tanzania in 1964 the child mortality rate was 335 deaths per 1,000 live births. Since independence the rate of child deaths declined to 62 per 1000 births.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><br> </p> 
      <table class="navbox"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th colspan="10" class="navbox-title">
          <div style="float:right; width:6em; height:2.6em">
           &nbsp;

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg/220px-Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg/330px-Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg/440px-Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hadzabe_Hunters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza</a> live as <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The population consists of <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_groups_in_Tanzania" title="List of ethnic groups in Tanzania">about 125 ethnic groups</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lev_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lev-195">[193]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sukuma_people" title="Sukuma people">Sukuma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyamwezi_people" title="Nyamwezi people">Nyamwezi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaga_people" title="Chaga people">Chagga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Haya_people" title="Haya people">Haya</a> peoples each have a population exceeding 1 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Otiso_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Otiso-196">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 4</span></sup> Approximately 99 per cent of Tanzanians are of native <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of Africa">African</a> descent, with small numbers of Arab, European, and Asian descent.<sup id="cite_ref-lev_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lev-195">[193]</a></sup> The majority of Tanzanians, including the Sukuma and the Nyamwezi, are <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The population also includes people of Arab and <a href="/wiki/Indians_in_Tanzania" title="Indians in Tanzania">Indian</a> origin, and small European and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Tanzania" title="Chinese people in Tanzania">Chinese</a> communities.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> Many also identify as <a href="/wiki/Shirazi_(ethnic_group)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shirazi (ethnic group)">Shirazis</a>. Thousands of Arabs and Indians were massacred during the <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Revolution" title="Zanzibar Revolution">Zanzibar Revolution</a> of 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-Zanzibar_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zanzibar-56">[54]</a></sup> As of 1994, the Asian community numbered 50,000 on the mainland and 4,000 on Zanzibar. An estimated 70,000 Arabs and 10,000 Europeans lived in Tanzania.<sup id="cite_ref-pop_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop-199">[197]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some <a href="/wiki/Albinos" class="mw-redirect" title="Albinos">albinos</a> in Tanzania have been the victims of violence in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> Attacks are often to hack off the limbs of albinos in the perverse superstitious belief that possessing the bones of albinos will bring wealth. The country has banned witch doctors to try to prevent the practice, but it has continued and albinos remain targets.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to 2010 Tanzanian government statistics, the <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> in Tanzania was 5.4 children born per woman, with 3.7 in urban mainland areas, 6.1 in rural mainland areas, and 5.1 in Zanzibar.<sup id="cite_ref-DHS_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-205">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 55</span></sup> For all women aged 45–49, 37.3 per cent had given birth to eight or more children, and for currently married women in that age group, 45.0 per cent had given birth to that many children.<sup id="cite_ref-DHS_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DHS-205">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>page 61</span></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanzania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Tanzania" title="Religion in Tanzania">Religion in Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Tanzania" title="Christianity in Tanzania">Christianity in Tanzania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Zanzibar" title="Islam in Zanzibar">Islam in Zanzibar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;"> 
       <div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:250px; background: white;">

          </tr>

          <tr>
           <td colspan="5" style="padding:5px; text-align:left;">Source: <a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-19">[17]</a></sup></td>

       </div> 
      </div>
      <p>Official statistics on religion are unavailable because religious surveys were eliminated from government census reports after 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> Tanzania's religious field is dominated by Christianity and Islam as well as of different African Traditional Religions connected to ethnic customs. The word for religion in Swahili, <i>dini,</i> generally apply to the world religions of Christianity and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> meaning that followers of African Traditional Religions are consider to be of "no religion". Religious belonging is often ambiguous, with some people adhering to multiple religious identities at the same time (for instance being Christian but also following African Traditional rituals) something which point to that religious boundaries are flexible and contextual.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to a 2014 estimate by the <i><a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a></i>, 61.4% of the population was Christian, 35.2% was Muslim, 1.8% practised traditional African religions, 1.4% were unaffiliated with any religion, and 0.2% followed other religions. Nearly the entire population of Zanzibar is Muslim.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-19">[17]</a></sup> Of Muslims, 16% are <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a>, 20% are <a href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-denominational Muslims">non-denominational Muslims</a>, 40% are <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a>, 20 per cent are <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a>, and 4% are <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Within the Christian community the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> is the largest denomination (51% all Christians).<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup> Among the Protestants, the large number of <a href="/wiki/Lutherans" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutherans">Lutherans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravians</a> points to the German missionary past of the country, while the number of <a href="/wiki/Anglicans" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglicans">Anglicans</a> point to the British missionary history of Tanganyika. A growing number have adopted <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Adventists" class="mw-redirect" title="Adventists">Adventists</a> likewise have an increasing presence because of external missionary activities from Scandinavia and the United States, especially during the first part of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup> All of them have had some influence in varying degrees from the Walokole movement (<a href="/wiki/East_African_Revival" title="East African Revival">East African Revival</a>), which has also been fertile ground for the spread of <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_Christianity" title="Charismatic Christianity">charismatic</a> and Pentecostal groups.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are also active communities of other religious groups, primarily on the mainland, such as <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Tanzania" title="Hinduism in Tanzania">Hindus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Bahá'ís</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> </p>
